# Text Elements
x.com ^7wtfzRaX

http://x.com ^5CiiWvNs

http ^gZyShVam

301 https ^REfCgeAE

https ^EShG8ubs

http ^bXxuoie1

Victim ^DOZ3i2bz

MITM ^0Q6imL6V

Victim has no SSL access ^TDnTGwxL

- SSL upgrade?

- Default SSL? ^fuMS4Nu0

https://x.com ^JkJcD7fV

x.com certificate ^TnMyc0Yk

CA ^pgDUzQBc

You have to check the certificate that you get!
Browser CA does this ^PJq77rdk

x.com sends its certificate
to CA (for CA to sign) ^iHQXMoxy

Signed x.com certificate ^hkrH3tOU

MITM can buy certificate ^fX6I4CQS

To prevent MITM buying certificate
CA has to do:

1) Domain based validation
There is a few ways for this

a) CA signs the certificate, but does not return it
directly.

Instead, CA sends it (certificate) to an email address, 
under that domain (ex: info@x.com)

This means that, if you were to hack x.com's mail server,
you can theoretically, have your certificate signed by CA

b) CA asks the owner to place a certain file,
under a certain directory (ex: https://x.com/verysecretfile.txt.)
and CA makes a GET request to web server ^z1ZueZ4y

x.com ^9g9az4pq

HSTS (HTTPS Strict Transport Security) ^MSnOcNAI

Forces HTTPS by default
So, even the first request is HTTPS

Without HSTS, first request is always HTTP,
with sometimes websites redirecting to HTTPS

Header Example:
strict-transport-security: max-age=14534536; includeSubDomains; preload ^flW5nnp2
